<p><b>Kubernetes</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span></span>(<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/j/: &#39;y&#39; in &#39;yes&#39;">j</span></span>)<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;b&#39; in &#39;buy&#39;">b</span><span title="/ər/: &#39;er&#39; in &#39;letter&#39;">ər</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>,<span class="wrap"> </span>-<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <b>K8s</b>)<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is an <a href="/wiki/Open-source_software" title="Open-source software">open-source</a> <a href="/wiki/OS-level_virtualization" title="OS-level virtualization">container</a> <a href="/wiki/Orchestration_(computing)" title="Orchestration (computing)">orchestration</a> system for automating <a href="/wiki/Software_deployment" title="Software deployment">software deployment</a>, scaling, and management.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Originally designed by <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, the project is now maintained by a worldwide community of contributors, and the trademark is held by the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Native_Computing_Foundation" title="Cloud Native Computing Foundation">Cloud Native Computing Foundation</a>.
</p><p>The name <i>Kubernetes</i> originates from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> κυβερνήτης (kubernḗtēs), meaning 'governor', '<a href="/wiki/Helmsman" title="Helmsman">helmsman</a>' or 'pilot'. <i>Kubernetes</i> is often abbreviated as <i>K8s</i>, counting the eight letters between the <i>K</i> and the <i>s</i> (a <a href="/wiki/Numeronym" title="Numeronym">numeronym</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Kubernetes assembles one or more computers, either <a href="/wiki/Virtual_machine" title="Virtual machine">virtual machines</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bare_machine" title="Bare machine">bare metal</a>, into a <a href="/wiki/Computer_cluster" title="Computer cluster">cluster</a> which can run workloads in containers. It works with various container runtimes, such as <a href="/wiki/Containerd" class="mw-redirect" title="Containerd">containerd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Native_Computing_Foundation#CRI-O" title="Cloud Native Computing Foundation">CRI-O</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its suitability for running and managing workloads of all sizes and styles has led to its widespread adoption in clouds and data centers. There are multiple distributions of this platform – from <a href="/wiki/Independent_software_vendor" title="Independent software vendor">independent software vendors (ISVs)</a> as well as hosted-on-cloud offerings from all the major public cloud vendors.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Kubernetes is one of the most widely deployed software systems in the world<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> being used across companies including <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cisco" title="Cisco">Cisco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_Inc." title="Apple Inc.">Apple</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meta_Platforms" title="Meta Platforms">Meta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reddit" title="Reddit">Reddit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinterest" title="Pinterest">Pinterest</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg/220px-Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg/330px-Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg/440px-Kubernetes_talk_at_Google_Cloud_Summit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a><figcaption>Google Kubernetes Engine talk at 2017 Google Cloud Summit</figcaption></figure>
<p>Kubernetes was announced by Google on June 6, 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The project was conceived and created by Google employees Joe Beda, Brendan Burns, and Craig McLuckie. Others at Google soon joined to help build the project including Ville Aikas, Dawn Chen, Brian Grant, Tim Hockin, and Daniel Smith.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other companies such as <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">Red Hat</a> and <a href="/wiki/CoreOS" class="mw-redirect" title="CoreOS">CoreOS</a> joined the effort soon after, with notable contributors such as Clayton Coleman and <a href="/wiki/Kelsey_Hightower" title="Kelsey Hightower">Kelsey Hightower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The design and development of Kubernetes was inspired by Google's <a href="/wiki/Borg_(cluster_manager)" title="Borg (cluster manager)">Borg</a> cluster manager and based on <a href="/wiki/Promise_theory" title="Promise theory">Promise Theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many of its top contributors had previously worked on Borg;<sup id="cite_ref-borg_paper_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-borg_paper-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> they codenamed Kubernetes "<span class="nowrap">Project 7</span>" after the <i>Star Trek</i> ex-<a href="/wiki/Borg" title="Borg">Borg</a> character <a href="/wiki/Seven_of_Nine" title="Seven of Nine">Seven of Nine</a><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and gave its logo a seven-spoked ship's wheel (designed by Tim Hockin). Unlike Borg, which was written in <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-borg_paper_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-borg_paper-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kubernetes is written in the <a href="/wiki/Go_(programming_language)" title="Go (programming language)">Go</a> language.
</p><p>Kubernetes was announced in June, 2014 and version 1.0 was released on July 21, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Google worked with the <a href="/wiki/Linux_Foundation" title="Linux Foundation">Linux Foundation</a> to form the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Native_Computing_Foundation" title="Cloud Native Computing Foundation">Cloud Native Computing Foundation</a> (CNCF)<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and offered Kubernetes as the seed technology.
</p><p>Google was already offering a managed Kubernetes service, <a href="/wiki/Google_Cloud_Platform" title="Google Cloud Platform">GKE</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">Red Hat</a> was supporting Kubernetes as part of <a href="/wiki/OpenShift" title="OpenShift">OpenShift</a> since the inception of the Kubernetes project in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2017, the principal competitors rallied around Kubernetes and announced adding native support for it:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/VMware" title="VMware">VMware</a> (proponent of <a href="/wiki/Pivotal_Cloud_Foundry" class="mw-redirect" title="Pivotal Cloud Foundry">Pivotal Cloud Foundry</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in August,</li>
<li>Mesosphere, Inc. (proponent of Marathon and <a href="/wiki/Apache_Mesos" title="Apache Mesos">Mesos</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in September,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Docker,_Inc." title="Docker, Inc.">Docker, Inc.</a> (proponent of <a href="/wiki/Docker_(software)" title="Docker (software)">Docker</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in October,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Azure" title="Microsoft Azure">Microsoft Azure</a><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> also in October,</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amazon_Web_Services" title="Amazon Web Services">AWS</a> announced support for Kubernetes via the Elastic Kubernetes Service (EKS)<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in November.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cisco" title="Cisco">Cisco</a> Elastic Kubernetes Service (EKS)<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in November.</li></ul>
<p>On March 6, 2018, Kubernetes Project reached ninth place in the list of <a href="/wiki/GitHub" title="GitHub">GitHub</a> projects by the number of <a href="/wiki/Commit_(version_control)" title="Commit (version control)">commits</a>, and second place in authors and issues, after the <a href="/wiki/Linux_kernel" title="Linux kernel">Linux kernel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Until version 1.18, Kubernetes followed an N-2 support policy, meaning that the three most recent minor versions receive security updates and bug fixes.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Starting with version 1.19, Kubernetes follows an N-3 support policy.<sup id="cite_ref-increase_to_N-3_support_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-increase_to_N-3_support-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Concepts">Concepts</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Concepts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Kubernetes.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Kubernetes.png/250px-Kubernetes.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="156" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Kubernetes.png/330px-Kubernetes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Kubernetes.png/500px-Kubernetes.png 2x" data-file-width="890" data-file-height="630" /></a><figcaption>Kubernetes architecture diagram</figcaption></figure>
<p>Kubernetes defines a set of building blocks ("primitives") that collectively provide mechanisms that deploy, maintain, and scale applications based on <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPU</a>, memory<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or custom metrics.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kubernetes is <a href="/wiki/Loose_coupling" title="Loose coupling">loosely coupled</a> and extensible to meet the needs of different workloads. The internal components as well as extensions and containers that run on Kubernetes rely on the Kubernetes API.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The platform exerts its control over compute and storage resources by defining resources as objects, which can then be managed as such.
</p><p>Kubernetes follows the <a href="/wiki/Primary/replica_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary/replica architecture">primary/replica architecture</a>. The components of Kubernetes can be divided into those that manage an individual <a href="/wiki/Node_(networking)" title="Node (networking)">node</a> and those that are part of the control plane.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Control_plane">Control plane</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Control plane"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Kubernetes master node handles the Kubernetes control plane of the cluster, managing its workload and directing communication across the system. The Kubernetes control plane consists of various components such as <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">TLS encryption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Role-based_access_control" title="Role-based access control">RBAC</a>, and a strong <a href="/wiki/Authentication" title="Authentication">authentication</a> method, network separation, each its own process, that can run both on a single master node or on multiple masters supporting <a href="/wiki/High-availability_cluster" title="High-availability cluster">high-availability clusters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The various components of the Kubernetes control plane are as follows.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Etcd">Etcd</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Etcd"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Etcd" class="mw-redirect" title="Etcd">Etcd</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a persistent, lightweight, distributed, <a href="/wiki/Key-value_data_store" class="mw-redirect" title="Key-value data store">key-value data store</a> (originally developed for <a href="/wiki/Container_Linux" title="Container Linux">Container Linux</a>). It reliably stores the configuration data of the cluster, representing the overall state of the cluster at any given point of time. Etcd favors consistency over availability in the event of a network partition (see <a href="/wiki/CAP_theorem" title="CAP theorem">CAP theorem</a>). The consistency is crucial for correctly scheduling and operating services.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="API_server">API server</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: API server"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <b>API server</b> serves the Kubernetes <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">API</a> using <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a> over <a href="/wiki/HTTP" title="HTTP">HTTP</a>, which provides both the internal and external interface to Kubernetes.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The API server processes, validates <a href="/wiki/REST" title="REST">REST</a> requests, and updates the state of the API objects in etcd, thereby allowing clients to configure workloads and containers across worker nodes.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The API server uses etcd's watch API to monitor the cluster, roll out critical configuration changes, or restore any divergences of the state of the cluster back to the desired state as declared in etcd.
</p><p>As an example, a human operator may specify that three instances of a particular "pod" (see below) need to be running, and etcd stores this fact. If the Deployment controller finds that only two instances are running (conflicting with the etcd declaration),<sup id="cite_ref-:6_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it schedules the creation of an additional instance of that pod.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Scheduler">Scheduler</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Scheduler"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <b>scheduler</b> is an extensible component that selects the node that an unscheduled pod (the basic unit of workloads to be scheduled) runs, based on resource availability and other constraints. The scheduler tracks resource allocation on each node to ensure that workload is not scheduled in excess of available resources. For this purpose, the scheduler must know the resource requirements, resource availability, and other user-provided constraints or policy directives such as quality-of-service, affinity/anti-affinity requirements, and data locality. The scheduler's role is to match resource "supply" to workload "demand".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Kubernetes allows running multiple schedulers within a single cluster. As such, scheduler <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_(computing)" title="Plug-in (computing)">plug-ins</a> may be developed and installed as in-process extensions to the native vanilla scheduler by running it as a separate scheduler, as long as they conform to the Kubernetes scheduling framework.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This allows cluster administrators to extend or modify the behavior of the default Kubernetes scheduler according to their needs.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Controllers">Controllers</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Controllers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <b>controller</b> is a reconciliation loop that drives the actual cluster state toward the desired state, communicating with the API server to create, update, and delete the resources it manages (e.g., pods or service endpoints).<sup id="cite_ref-coreos-replication-controller_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coreos-replication-controller-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:1_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An example controller is a ReplicaSet controller, which handles replication and scaling by running a specified number of copies of a pod across the cluster. The controller also handles creating replacement pods if the underlying node fails.<sup id="cite_ref-coreos-replication-controller_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coreos-replication-controller-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other controllers that are part of the core Kubernetes system include a DaemonSet controller for running exactly one pod on every machine (or some subset of machines), and a Job controller for running pods that run to completion (e.g. as part of a batch job).<sup id="cite_ref-exciting-experimental-features_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-exciting-experimental-features-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Labels selectors often form part of the controller's definition that specify the set of pods that a controller manages.<sup id="cite_ref-redhat-docker-and-kubernetes-training-labels-examples_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redhat-docker-and-kubernetes-training-labels-examples-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <b>controller manager</b> is a single process that manages several core Kubernetes controllers (including the examples described above), is distributed as part of the standard Kubernetes installation and responding to the loss of nodes.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Custom controllers may also be installed in the cluster, further allowing the behavior and API of Kubernetes to be extended when used in conjunction with custom resources (see <a href="#Custom_resources,_controllers_and_operators">custom resources, controllers and operators</a> below).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nodes">Nodes</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Nodes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A node, also known as a worker or a minion, is a machine where containers (workloads) are deployed. Every node in the cluster must run a container <a href="/wiki/Runtime_system" title="Runtime system">runtime</a>, as well as the below-mentioned components, for communication with the primary network configuration of these containers.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="kubelet">kubelet</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: kubelet"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b>kubelet</b> is responsible for the running state of each node, ensuring that all containers on the node are healthy. It takes care of starting, stopping, and maintaining application containers organized into pods as directed by the control plane.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> kubelet monitors the state of a pod, and if not in the desired state, the pod re-deploys to the same node. Node status is relayed every few seconds via heartbeat messages to the API server. Once the control plane detects a node failure, a higher-level controller is expected to observe this state change and launch pods on another healthy node.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Container_runtime">Container runtime</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Container runtime"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <b>container runtime</b> is responsible for the lifecycle of containers, including launching, reconciling and killing of containers. kubelet interacts with container runtimes via the Container Runtime Interface (CRI),<sup id="cite_ref-:4_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which <a href="/wiki/Coupling_(computer_programming)" title="Coupling (computer programming)">decouples</a> the maintenance of core Kubernetes from the actual CRI implementation.
</p><p>Originally, kubelet interfaced exclusively with the <a href="/wiki/Docker_(software)" title="Docker (software)">Docker</a> runtime<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> through a "dockershim". However, from November 2020<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> up to April 2022, Kubernetes has deprecated the <a href="/wiki/Shim_(computing)" title="Shim (computing)">shim</a> in favor of directly interfacing with the container through containerd, or replacing Docker with a runtime that is compliant with the Container Runtime Interface (CRI).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the release of v1.24 in May 2022, the "dockershim" has been removed entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Examples of popular container runtimes that are compatible with kubelet include <a href="/wiki/Containerd" class="mw-redirect" title="Containerd">containerd</a> (initially supported via Docker) and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Native_Computing_Foundation#CRI-O" title="Cloud Native Computing Foundation">CRI-O</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="kube-proxy">kube-proxy</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: kube-proxy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b>kube-proxy</b> is an implementation of a <a href="/wiki/Proxy_server" title="Proxy server">network proxy</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Load_balancing_(computing)" title="Load balancing (computing)">load balancer</a>, and it supports the service abstraction along with the other networking operations.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is responsible for routing traffic to the appropriate container based on IP and port number of the incoming request.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Namespaces">Namespaces</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Namespaces"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In Kubernetes, <b>namespaces</b> are utilized to segregate the resources it handles into distinct and non-intersecting collections.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are intended for use in environments with many users spread across multiple teams, or projects, or even separating environments like development, test, and production.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pods">Pods</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Pods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The basic scheduling unit in Kubernetes is a <b>pod</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which consists of one or more containers that are guaranteed to be co-located on the same node.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each pod in Kubernetes is assigned a unique IP address within the cluster, allowing applications to use ports without the risk of conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-kubernetes-101-networking_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kubernetes-101-networking-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Within the pod, all containers can reference each other.
</p><p>A <b>container</b> resides inside a pod. The container is the lowest level of a micro-service, which holds the running application, libraries, and their dependencies.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Workloads">Workloads</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Workloads"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Kubernetes supports several abstractions of workloads that are at a higher level over simple pods. This allows users to declaratively define and manage these high-level abstractions, instead of having to manage individual pods by themselves. Several of these abstractions, supported by a standard installation of Kubernetes, are described below.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="ReplicaSets,_ReplicationControllers_and_Deployments"><span id="ReplicaSets.2C_ReplicationControllers_and_Deployments"></span>ReplicaSets, ReplicationControllers and Deployments</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: ReplicaSets, ReplicationControllers and Deployments"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A <b>ReplicaSet</b>'s purpose is to maintain a stable set of replica pods running at any given time. As such, it is often used to guarantee the availability of a specified number of identical Pods.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ReplicaSet can also be said to be a grouping mechanism that lets Kubernetes maintain the number of instances that have been declared for a given pod. The definition of a ReplicaSet uses a selector, whose evaluation will result in identifying all pods that are associated with it.
</p><p>A <b>ReplicationController</b>, similar to a ReplicaSet, serves the same purpose and behaves similarly to a ReplicaSet, which is to ensure that there will always be a specified number of pod replicas as desired. The ReplicationController workload was the predecessor of a ReplicaSet, but was eventually deprecated in favor of ReplicaSet to make use of set-based label selectors.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><b>Deployments</b> are a higher-level management mechanism for ReplicaSets. While the ReplicaSet controller manages the scale of the ReplicaSet, the Deployment controller manages what happens to the ReplicaSet – whether an update has to be rolled out, or rolled back, etc. When Deployments are scaled up or down, this results in the declaration of the ReplicaSet changing, and this change in the declared state is managed by the ReplicaSet controller.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="StatefulSets">StatefulSets</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: StatefulSets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b>StatefulSets</b> are controllers that enforce the properties of uniqueness and ordering amongst instances of a pod, and can be used to run <a href="/wiki/State_(computer_science)" title="State (computer science)">stateful</a> applications.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While scaling stateless applications is only a matter of adding more running pods, doing so for stateful workloads is harder, because the state needs to be preserved if a pod is restarted. If the application is scaled up or down, the state may need to be redistributed.
</p><p>Databases are an example of stateful workloads. When run in high-availability mode, many databases come with the notion of a primary instance and secondary instances. In this case, the notion of ordering of instances is important. Other applications like <a href="/wiki/Apache_Kafka" title="Apache Kafka">Apache Kafka</a> distribute the data amongst their brokers; hence, one broker is not the same as another. In this case, the notion of instance uniqueness is important.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="DaemonSets">DaemonSets</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: DaemonSets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><b>DaemonSets</b> are responsible for ensuring that a pod is created on every single node in the cluster.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Generally, most workloads scale in response to a desired replica count, depending on the availability and performance requirements as needed by the application. However, in other scenarios it may be necessary to deploy a pod to every single node in the cluster, scaling up the number of total pods as nodes are added and <a href="/wiki/Garbage_collection_(computer_science)" title="Garbage collection (computer science)">garbage collecting</a> them as they are removed. This is particularly helpful for use cases where the workload has some dependency on the actual node or host machine, such as log collection, ingress controllers, and storage services.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Services">Services</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Services"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Pod-networking.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pod-networking.png/220px-Pod-networking.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="97" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pod-networking.png/330px-Pod-networking.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Pod-networking.png/440px-Pod-networking.png 2x" data-file-width="881" data-file-height="389" /></a><figcaption>Simplified view showing how Services interact with Pod networking in a Kubernetes cluster</figcaption></figure>
<p>A Kubernetes <b>service</b> is a set of pods that work together, such as one tier of a <a href="/wiki/Multitier_architecture" title="Multitier architecture">multi-tier</a> application. The set of pods that constitute a service are defined by a label selector.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kubernetes provides two modes of <a href="/wiki/Service_discovery" title="Service discovery">service discovery</a>, using environment variables or using Kubernetes DNS.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Service discovery assigns a stable IP address and <a href="/wiki/DNS_name" class="mw-redirect" title="DNS name">DNS name</a> to the service, and load balances traffic in a <a href="/wiki/Round-robin_DNS" title="Round-robin DNS">round-robin</a> manner to network connections of that IP address among the pods matching the selector (even as failures cause the pods to move from machine to machine).<sup id="cite_ref-kubernetes-101-networking_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kubernetes-101-networking-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By default a service is exposed inside a cluster (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Front_and_back_ends" class="mw-redirect" title="Front and back ends">back end</a> pods might be grouped into a service, with requests from the front-end pods load-balanced among them), but a service can also be exposed outside a cluster (e.g., for clients to reach front-end pods).<sup id="cite_ref-kubernetes-101-external-access_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kubernetes-101-external-access-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Volumes">Volumes</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Volumes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/File_system" title="File system">Filesystems</a> in the Kubernetes container provide <a href="/wiki/Ephemeral_storage" class="mw-redirect" title="Ephemeral storage">ephemeral storage</a>, by default. This means that a restart of the pod will wipe out any data on such containers, and therefore, this form of storage is quite limiting in anything but trivial applications. A Kubernetes <b>volume</b><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> provides persistent storage that exists for the lifetime of the pod itself. This storage can also be used as shared disk space for containers within the pod. Volumes are mounted at specific mount points within the container, which are defined by the pod configuration, and cannot mount onto other volumes or link to other volumes. The same volume can be mounted at different points in the file system tree by different containers.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="ConfigMaps_and_Secrets">ConfigMaps and Secrets</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: ConfigMaps and Secrets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A common application challenge is deciding where to store and manage configuration information, some of which may contain sensitive data. Configuration data can be anything as fine-grained as individual properties, or coarse-grained information like entire configuration files such as <a href="/wiki/JSON" title="JSON">JSON</a> or <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a> documents. Kubernetes provides two closely related mechanisms to deal with this need, known as <b>ConfigMaps</b> and <b>Secrets</b>, both of which allow for configuration changes to be made without requiring an application rebuild.
</p><p>The data from ConfigMaps and Secrets will be made available to every single instance of the application to which these objects have been bound via the Deployment. A Secret and/or a ConfigMap is sent to a node only if a pod on that node requires it, which will only be stored in memory on the node. Once the pod that depends on the Secret or ConfigMap is deleted, the in-memory copy of all bound Secrets and ConfigMaps are deleted as well.
</p><p>The data from a ConfigMap or Secret is accessible to the pod through one of the following ways:<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>As <a href="/wiki/Environment_variable" title="Environment variable">environment variables</a>, which will be consumed by kubelet from the ConfigMap when the container is launched;</li>
<li>Mounted within a volume accessible within the container's filesystem, which supports automatic reloading without restarting the container.</li></ol>
<p>The biggest difference between a Secret and a ConfigMap is that Secrets are specifically designed for containing secure and confidential data, although they are not <a href="/wiki/Data_at_rest#Encryption" title="Data at rest">encrypted at rest</a> by default, and requires additional setup in order to fully secure the use of Secrets within the cluster.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Secrets are often used to store confidential or sensitive data like certificates, credentials to work with image registries, passwords, and <a href="/wiki/Secure_Shell" title="Secure Shell">ssh</a> keys.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Labels_and_selectors">Labels and selectors</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Labels and selectors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Kubernetes enables clients (users or internal components) to attach keys called <b>labels</b> to any API object in the system, such as pods and <a href="#Kubernetes_node">nodes</a>. Correspondingly, <b>label selectors</b> are queries against labels that resolve to matching objects.<sup id="cite_ref-do-intro_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-do-intro-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When a service is defined, one can define the label selectors that will be used by the service router/load balancer to select the pod instances that the traffic will be routed to. Thus, simply changing the labels of the pods or changing the label selectors on the service can be used to control which pods get traffic and which don't, which can be used to support various deployment patterns like <a href="/wiki/Blue%E2%80%93green_deployment" title="Blue–green deployment">blue–green deployments</a> or <a href="/wiki/A/B_testing" title="A/B testing">A/B testing</a>. This capability to dynamically control how services utilize implementing resources provides a loose coupling within the infrastructure.
</p><p>For example, if an application's pods have labels for a system <code>tier</code> (with values such as <code>frontend</code>, <code>backend</code>, for example) and a <code>release_track</code> (with values such as <code><a href="/wiki/Feature_toggle#Canary_release" title="Feature toggle">canary</a></code>, <code>production</code>, for example), then an operation on all of <code>backend</code> and <code>canary</code> nodes can use a label selector, such as:<sup id="cite_ref-redhat-docker-and-kubernetes-training-labels-examples_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-redhat-docker-and-kubernetes-training-labels-examples-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p><code>tier=backend AND release_track=canary</code></p></blockquote>
<p>Just like labels, field selectors also let one select Kubernetes resources. Unlike labels, the selection is based on the attribute values inherent to the resource being selected, rather than user-defined categorization. <code>metadata.name</code> and <code>metadata.namespace</code> are field selectors that will be present on all Kubernetes objects. Other selectors that can be used depend on the object/resource type.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Add-ons">Add-ons</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Add-ons"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Add-ons are additional features of the Kubernetes cluster implemented as applications running within it. The pods may be managed by Deployments, ReplicationControllers, and so on. There are many add-ons. Some of the more important are:
</p>
<dl><dt>DNS</dt>
<dd>Cluster DNS is a DNS server, in addition to the other DNS server(s) in the environment, which serves DNS records for Kubernetes services. Containers started by Kubernetes automatically include this DNS server in their DNS searches.</dd>
<dt>Web UI</dt>
<dd>This is a general purpose, web-based UI for Kubernetes clusters. It allows administrators to manage and troubleshoot applications running in the cluster, as well as the cluster itself.</dd>
<dt>Resource monitoring</dt>
<dd>Container Resource Monitoring records metrics about containers in a central database, and provides a UI for browsing that data.</dd>
<dt>Cost monitoring</dt>
<dd>Kubernetes cost monitoring applications allow breakdown of costs by pods, nodes, namespaces, and labels.</dd>
<dt>Cluster-level logging</dt>
<dd>To prevent the loss of event data in the event of node or pod failures, container logs can be saved to a central log store with a search/browsing interface. Kubernetes provides no native storage for log data, but one can integrate many existing logging solutions into the Kubernetes cluster.</dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Storage">Storage</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Storage"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Containers emerged as a way to make software portable. The container contains all the packages needed to run a service. The provided file system makes containers extremely portable and easy to use in development. A container can be moved from development to test or production with no or relatively few configuration changes.
</p><p>Historically Kubernetes was suitable only for stateless services. However, many applications have a database, which requires persistence, which leads to the creation of persistent storage for Kubernetes. Implementing persistent storage for containers is one of the top challenges of Kubernetes administrators, DevOps and cloud engineers. Containers may be ephemeral, but more and more of their data is not, so one needs to ensure the data's survival in case of container termination or hardware failure. When deploying containers with Kubernetes or containerized applications, organizations often realize that they need persistent storage. They need to provide fast and reliable storage for databases, root images and other data used by the containers.
</p><p>In addition to the landscape, the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Native_Computing_Foundation" title="Cloud Native Computing Foundation">Cloud Native Computing Foundation</a> (CNCF), has published other information about Kubernetes Persistent Storage including a blog helping to define the container attached storage pattern. This pattern can be thought of as one that uses Kubernetes itself as a component of the storage system or service.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>More information about the relative popularity of these and other approaches can be found on the CNCF's landscape survey as well, which showed that OpenEBS – a Stateful Persistent Storage platform from Datacore Software,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Rook – a storage orchestration project – were the two projects most likely to be in evaluation as of the Fall of 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Container Attached Storage is a type of data storage that emerged as Kubernetes gained prominence. The Container Attached Storage approach or pattern relies on Kubernetes itself for certain capabilities while delivering primarily block, file, object and interfaces to workloads running on Kubernetes.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Common attributes of Container Attached Storage include the use of extensions to Kubernetes, such as custom resource definitions, and the use of Kubernetes itself for functions that otherwise would be separately developed and deployed for storage or data management. Examples of functionality delivered by custom resource definitions or by Kubernetes itself include retry logic, delivered by Kubernetes itself, and the creation and maintenance of an inventory of available storage media and volumes, typically delivered via a custom resource definition.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Container_Storage_Interface_(CSI)"><span id="Container_Storage_Interface_.28CSI.29"></span>Container Storage Interface (CSI)</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Container Storage Interface (CSI)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In Kubernetes version 1.9, the initial Alpha release of Container Storage Interface (CSI) was introduced.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Previously, storage volume plug-ins were included in the Kubernetes distribution. By creating a standardized CSI, the code required to interface with external storage systems was separated from the core Kubernetes code base. Just one year later, the CSI feature was made Generally Available (GA) in Kubernetes.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="API">API</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: API"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A key component of the Kubernetes control plane is the API Server, which exposes an HTTP API that can be invoked by other parts of the cluster as well as end users and external components. This API is a <a href="/wiki/REST" title="REST">REST</a> API and is declarative in nature, and is the same API exposed to the control plane.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The API server is backed by etcd to store all records persistently.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="API_objects">API objects</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: API objects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In Kubernetes, all objects serve as the "<a href="/wiki/Intent_log" title="Intent log">record of intent</a>" of the cluster's state, and are able to define the desired state that the writer of the object wishes for the cluster to be in.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As such, most Kubernetes objects have the same set of nested fields, as follows:
</p>
<ul><li><code>spec</code>: Describes the desired state of the resource, which can be controlled by end users, or other higher-level controllers;</li>
<li><code>status</code>: Describes the current state of the resource, which is actively updated by the controller of the resource.</li></ul>
<p>All objects in Kubernetes are subject to the same API conventions. Some of these include:
</p>
<ul><li>Must have the following metadata under the nested object field <code>metadata</code>:<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<ul><li><code>namespace</code>: a label that objects are subdivided into;</li>
<li><code>name</code>: a string that uniquely identifies the object within the defined namespace;</li>
<li><code>uid</code>: a unique string that is able to distinguish between objects with the same name across space and time (even across deletions and recreations with the same name).</li></ul></li>
<li>May be managed by another controller, which is defined in the <code>metadata.ownerReferences</code> field:<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<ul><li>At most one other object shall be the managing controller of the controllee object, which is defined by the <code>controller</code> field.</li></ul></li>
<li>May be garbage collected if the owner is deleted:<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
<ul><li>When an object is deleted, all dependent objects may also be deleted in a cascading fashion.</li></ul></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Custom_resources,_controllers_and_operators"><span id="Custom_resources.2C_controllers_and_operators"></span>Custom resources, controllers and operators</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Custom resources, controllers and operators"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Kubernetes API can be extended using <i>Custom Resources</i>, which represent objects that are not part of the standard Kubernetes installation. These custom resources are declared using <i>Custom Resource Definitions</i> (CRDs), which is a kind of resource that can be dynamically registered and unregistered without shutting down or restarting a cluster that is currently running.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Custom controllers are another extension mechanism that interact with the Kubernetes API, similar to the default controllers in the standard pre-installed Kubernetes controller manager. These controllers may interact with custom resources to allow for a declarative API: users may declare the desired state of the system via the custom resources, and it is the responsibility of the custom controller to observe the change and reconcile it.
</p><p>The combination of custom resources and custom controllers are often referred to as a <i>Kubernetes</i> <i>Operator</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The key use case for operators are to capture the aim of a human operator who is managing a service or set of services and to implement them using automation, and with a declarative API supporting this automation. Human operators who look after specific applications and services have deep knowledge of how the system ought to behave, how to deploy it, and how to react if there are problems.
</p><p>Examples of problems solved by operators include taking and restoring backups of that application's state, and handling upgrades of the application code alongside related changes such as database schemas or extra configuration settings. Several notable projects under the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_Native_Computing_Foundation" title="Cloud Native Computing Foundation">Cloud Native Computing Foundation</a>'s incubation program follow the operator pattern to extend Kubernetes, including Argo, Open Policy Agent and Istio.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="API_security">API security</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: API security"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Kubernetes defines the following strategies for controlling access to its API.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Transport_security">Transport security</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Transport security"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Kubernetes API server listens on a <a href="/wiki/Transmission_Control_Protocol" title="Transmission Control Protocol">TCP port</a> that serves <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a> traffic, in order to enforce <a href="/wiki/Transport_Layer_Security" title="Transport Layer Security">transport layer security (TLS)</a> using <a href="/wiki/Certificate_authority" title="Certificate authority">CA certificates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In older versions of Kubernetes, the API server supported listening on both HTTP and HTTPS ports (with the HTTP port number having no transport security whatsoever). This was deprecated in v1.10 and eventually dropped support in v1.20 of Kubernetes.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Authentication">Authentication</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Authentication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>All requests made to the Kubernetes API server are expected to be authenticated, and supports several authentication strategies, some of which are listed below:<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/X.509" title="X.509">X.509</a> client certificates</li>
<li>Bearer tokens</li>
<li>Service account tokens, intended for programmatic API access</li></ol>
<p>Users are typically expected to indicate and define cluster URL details along with the necessary credentials in a <i>kubeconfig</i> file, which are natively supported by other Kubernetes tools like kubectl and the official Kubernetes client libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Authorization">Authorization</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Authorization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Kubernetes API supports the following authorization modes:<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li><i>Node authorization mode</i>: Grants a fixed list of operations of API requests that kubelets are allowed to perform, in order to function properly.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Attribute-based_access_control" title="Attribute-based access control">Attribute-based access control (ABAC)</a> mode</i>: Grants access rights to users through the use of defined access control policies which combine attributes together.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Role-based_access_control" title="Role-based access control">Role-based access control (RBAC)</a> mode</i>: Grants access rights to users based on roles that are granted to the user, where each role defines a list of actions that are allowed.</li>
<li><i>Webhook mode</i>: Queries a REST API service to determine if a user is authorized to perform a given action.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ol>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="API_clients">API clients</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: API clients"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Kubernetes supports several official API clients:
</p>
<ul><li>kubectl: Command-line for interacting with the Kubernetes control plane<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Official client libraries maintained by Kubernetes for <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a>, <a href="/wiki/.NET" title=".NET">.NET</a>, <a href="/wiki/Go_(programming_language)" title="Go (programming language)">Go</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haskell" title="Haskell">Haskell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java_(programming_language)" title="Java (programming language)">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perl" title="Perl">Perl</a>, <a href="/wiki/Python_(programming_language)" title="Python (programming language)">Python</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ruby_(programming_language)" title="Ruby (programming language)">Ruby</a><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cluster_API">Cluster API</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Cluster API"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The same API design principles have been used to define an API to harness a program in order to create, configure, and manage Kubernetes clusters. This is called the Cluster API.<sup id="cite_ref-Cluster_API_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cluster_API-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A key concept embodied in the API is using <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure_as_Software" class="mw-redirect" title="Infrastructure as Software">Infrastructure as Software</a>, or the notion that the Kubernetes cluster infrastructure is itself a resource / object that can be managed just like any other Kubernetes resources. Similarly, machines that make up the cluster are also treated as a Kubernetes resource. The API has two pieces – the core API, and a provider implementation. The provider implementation consists of cloud-provider specific functions that let Kubernetes provide the cluster API in a fashion that is well-integrated with the cloud-provider's services and resources.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Uses">Uses</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Uses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Kubernetes is commonly used as a way to host a <a href="/wiki/Microservice" class="mw-redirect" title="Microservice">microservice</a>-based implementation, because it and its associated ecosystem of tools provide all the capabilities needed to address key concerns of any <a href="/wiki/Microservices#A_comparison_of_platforms" title="Microservices">microservice architecture</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Criticism">Criticism</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Criticism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A common criticism of Kubernetes is that it is too complex. Google admitted this as well.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Distributions">Distributions</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Distributions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Various vendors offer Kubernetes-based platforms or <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure_as_a_service" title="Infrastructure as a service">infrastructure as a service</a> (IaaS) that deploy Kubernetes.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These are typically categorized according to open-source, commercial or managed distributions. Several notable distributions are listed below:<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Open-source_distributions">Open-source distributions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Open-source distributions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Amazon_(company)" title="Amazon (company)">Amazon</a> EKS-D</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://k0sproject.io/">k0s</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://k3s.io/">k3s</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/SUSE_S.A." title="SUSE S.A.">SUSE</a> Rancher Kubernetes Engine (RKE)</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.okd.io/">OKD.IO</a> The Community Distribution of Kubernetes that powers Red Hat OpenShift</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Commercial_distributions">Commercial distributions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Commercial distributions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li>D2iQ Kubernetes Platform</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mirantis" title="Mirantis">Mirantis</a> Kubernetes Engine (formerly <a href="/wiki/Docker,_Inc." title="Docker, Inc.">Docker</a> Enterprise)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Red_Hat" title="Red Hat">Red Hat</a> <a href="/wiki/OpenShift" title="OpenShift">OpenShift</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/VMware" title="VMware">VMware</a> Tanzu</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Managed_distributions">Managed distributions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kubernetes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Managed distributions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Alibaba_Cloud" title="Alibaba Cloud">Alibaba Cloud</a> ACK (Alibaba Cloud Container Service for Kubernetes)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Huawei" title="Huawei">Huawei</a> CCE (Huawei Cloud Container Engine)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> Cloud Kubernetes Services</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> AKS (Azure Kubernetes Services)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mirantis" title="Mirantis">Mirantis</a> Kubernetes Engine with OpsCare Plus managed services</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oracle_Cloud" title="Oracle Cloud">Oracle</a> Container Engine for Kubernetes</li>
<li>Platform9 Managed Kubernetes</li>
